Why Speech Cannot Exist Without a Locus
Nasafi proves speech requires a locus via absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speech (kalam) as an attribute cannot subsist without a locus (mahall), and the rational absurdities that follow from claiming otherwise.

Core Idea
Speech without a locus makes no being truly a speaker.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla tackles a critical issue in kalam: can speech exist as an attribute without inhering in a subject? Nasafi demonstrates that such a claim leads to logical contradictions and theological errors. Follow his reasoning to see how he defends the understanding that Allah's speech is an attribute subsisting in His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noting its flow. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the repeated 'لو جاز' (if it were permissible).
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins with 'على أن الكلام لو جاز وجوده لا في محل' — this is the premise being refuted. Identify the conclusion the author wants to reject.
3
Follow the consequence chain
Nasafi shows that if speech could exist without a locus, then no essence would be speaking with it (since none is more worthy). Then he extends the absurdity to motion, rest, colors, and all accidents.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Understand the theological stakes
He then refutes the view that God and creation share one speech, which would mix divine and created attributes. Finally, he states that speech without a speaker or a speaker without speech is impossible, and dismissing that is kufr.
This shows the seriousness of the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن الكلام لو جاز وجوده لا في محل لم يكن ذات ما متكلما به إذ ليس ذات بأولى بالاتصاف به من ذات ويستحيل أن تصير الذوات كلها متكلمة بكلام واحد، ولو جاز هذا، لجاز أن تكون جميع الأجسام متحركة بحركة واحدة ساكنة بسكون واحد، وكذا هذا في السواد والبياض وجميع الأعراض مع على أحد فساد قول من يقول الله تعالى وجميع خلقه موصوفون بكلام واحد فكان عين ما هو صفة الله تعالى صفة المحدثين، وعين ما هو صفتهم صفة الله تعالى، وكذا يستحيل أن لا يصير ذات ما موصوفا بالكلام الذي وجد لا في محل لأن فيه إخراج الكلام من أن يكون كلام الله، وإخراج ذات الله تعالى من أن يكون متكلما آمرا ناهيا، وهو كفر، على أن القول بكلام لا يكون ذات ما متكلما به محال وكذا متكلم ما من غير أن يكون له كلام. وبالله التوفيق.
That if speech were permissible to exist not in a locus, no essence would be speaking with it, because no essence is more worthy of being described with it than another essence; and it is impossible that all essences become speaking with one speech. And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible that all bodies be moving with one motion and at rest with one rest. Likewise, this applies to blackness and whiteness and all accidents, with the corruption of the statement of the one who says that God Most High and all His creation are described with one speech, so that which is the attribute of God Most High would become the attribute of the originated beings, and that which is their attribute would become the attribute of God Most High. Likewise, it is impossible that an essence not become described by the speech that exists not in a locus, because in that is the removal of speech from being the speech of God, and the removal of the essence of God Most High from being a speaker, commander, and prohibiter, and that is disbelief. Moreover, the statement of speech without any essence being a speaker with it is impossible, and likewise a speaker without having speech is impossible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the possibility of speech (kalam) existing without a locus (mahall). The author argues that if speech could exist in no place, then no particular essence (dhat) would be described as speaking with it, because no essence is more entitled to be described with it than another. This leads to the absurdity of all essences being speaking with one speech, or none. Similarly, it would imply that all bodies could be moving with one motion and at rest with one rest, which is impossible. This refutes the view that God and all creation are described with one and the same speech, making God's attribute the same as creatures' attributes and vice versa. It also leads to the impossibility of any essence being described with that speech, which would remove it from being God's speech and God from being a commanding and forbidding speaker—this is disbelief. The author concludes that the claim of speech without a speaker or a speaker without speech is impossible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila belief that speech (kalam) can exist as an accident without inhering in a subject (i.e., God's speech is created and not an attribute of His essence).
Correction: If speech existed without a locus, no being would be speaking with it, leading to absurdities and ultimately denying God's attribute of speech.

A) All bodies would move with one motion.
B) No essence would be speaking with that speech.
C) God's speech would become identical to created speech.
D) Speech would become eternal.

Key Takeaway
Speech must subsist in a locus; otherwise, it cannot be attributed to any being, leading to theological absurdities and disbelief.

?
The analogy with motion and rest shows that you naturally apply logical consistency to physical things. Impossibility of Speech Subsisting in a Locus Other Than God
The author refutes the third division: speech occurring in a locus other than God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speech cannot subsist in a locus other than God's essence, and how this refutes the third division.

Core Idea
A derived name from an attribute belongs to its locus, not its originator.

This lesson presents the refutation of the claim that God's speech could occur in a creature. The author argues that if speech occurred in a locus, that locus would be the speaker, not God, because attributes belong to their locus. He supports this with examples and a reference to a theological precedent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow from the third division to the conclusion.
2
Identify the key principle
The author states that a derived name from an attribute refers to the locus of the attribute, not its originator. This is the core of the argument.
3
Analyze the examples and parallel
The examples (dead, one-eyed, etc.) and the reference to Abu al-Hudhayl illustrate the principle. See how they apply to speech.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends by stating the nullity of all three divisions. Be sure to understand how this refutation eliminates the possibility of speech in a locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان امتناع القسم الثالث]: وإما القسم الثالث وهو حدوث الكلام في محل سوى ذات القديم فمحال أيضا، لأن الكلام لو حدث في محل لكان المتكلم الآمر الناهي المخبر ذلك المحل لا الله تعالى لأن الاسم المشتق عن الصفة يكون راجعا إلى محل الصفة لا إلى محدثه، والصفة تكون صفة لمحلها لا لمحدثها ألا ترى أن الميت والأعور والأعمى والأشل، والأعرج، والأسود والأبيض، والحلو والمر، والمتحرك والساكن محال هذه الصفات لا موجودها، ومن وصف موجود هذه الصفات بها تسارع الناس إلى إكفاره وقصدوا ضرب علاوته وإتلاف مهجته فكذا هذا. ولهذا زعم أبو الهذيل العلاف أن من خلق الله فيه الإيمان بطريق الجبر كان المؤمن هو المحل لا الله تعالى. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك علم أن الله تعالى ولو خلق الكلام في محل لكان المتكلم ذلك المحل لا الله تعالى. وظهر بهذا فساد الأقسام الثلاثة كلها.
Explanation of the impossibility of the third division: As for the third division, which is the occurrence of speech in a locus other than the essence of the Eternal, it is also impossible, because if speech occurred in a locus, then the speaker, commanding, forbidding, informing would be that locus, not God Most High, because the name derived from an attribute refers to the locus of the attribute, not to its originator, and the attribute is an attribute of its locus, not of its originator. Do you not see that the dead, the one-eyed, the blind, the palsied, the lame, the black, the white, the sweet, the bitter, the moving, and the at rest are loci of these attributes, not their bringers into existence? And if one were to describe the bringer into existence of these attributes with them, people would hasten to declare him a disbeliever and would aim to strike his prominence and destroy his life; likewise this is the case here. It is for this reason that Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf held that if God creates faith in someone by way of compulsion, the believer is the locus, not God Most High. And if the matter is thus, it is known that God Most High, even if He created speech in a locus, the speaker would be that locus, not God Most High. And by this the nullity of all three divisions becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the third division concerning God's speech: that it occurs in a locus other than God's essence. The author argues that if speech occurs in a locus, then that locus becomes the speaker—the one who commands, forbids, and informs—not God. The reason is that a derived name (al-ism al-mushtaqq) from an attribute refers to the locus of that attribute, not to its originator. The attribute belongs to its locus, not to the one who brought it into existence. The author provides examples: a dead person, one-eyed, blind, etc. are loci of these attributes, not the one who created them. If someone attributed these qualities to the Creator, people would consider that disbelief. Likewise, Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf held that if God compels faith in a person, the believer is the locus of faith, not God. Thus, if God created speech in a locus, that locus would be the speaker, not God. This demonstrates the falsehood of all three divisions (speech without a locus, speech in a locus other than God, and the third which is being refuted).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates speech in a prophet or angel, that being becomes the speaker of God's speech.
Correction: The author clarifies that the attribute belongs to its locus, not its originator. Thus, if speech occurs in a locus, that locus is the speaker, not God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why would a locus of speech become the speaker instead of God?
A) Because the locus is more deserving of the attribute.
B) Because a derived name from an attribute refers to the locus of the attribute, not its originator.
C) Because God cannot be directly described with speech.
D) Because the originator of speech is not the one who speaks.

Key Takeaway
Speech cannot subsist in a locus other than God because the derived name from an attribute belongs to its locus, making that locus the speaker, not God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever entertain the thought that its words are merely created or that they subsist in the book or your voice? In light of this lesson, what does your answer reveal about your understanding of God's speech?
?
The author uses the example of ascribing attributes like blindness to the creator as akin to disbelief. Have you ever loosely described God as 'speaking' in a way that implies His speech is created or separate from His essence? How will you guard your tongue after this?
?
Abu al-Hudhayl's example about faith being created in a person shows that the locus receives the attribute without being the originator. In your worship, do you ever attribute your own actions to yourself as if you are the originator, forgetting that you are merely a locus for God's creation? How can you remind yourself daily that all attributes of good in you are from God, and you are not their originator?

Affirming God's Speech as an Attribute
The author presents two alternatives regarding God's speech and critiques Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying God's speech as an attribute leads to logical contradictions and undermines God's justice.

Core Idea
Denying God’s speech makes His justice impossible.

The author presents a powerful dilemma: either God's speech is an eternal attribute, or faith and worship lose their basis. He then shows that denying speech forces the Mu'tazila to contradict their own principles of justice. This passage establishes that affirming divine speech as an attribute is necessary for coherent theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from the two alternatives to the critique.
The text ends abruptly with 'because' (li-anna), but the argument is essentially complete.
2
Identify the two alternatives
Find where the author presents the two positions: either affirm God's speech as an attribute, or deny it. Note the consequences of each.
The first is the position of Ahl al-Haqq; the second leads to kufr.
3
Examine the critique of Mu'tazila
See how the author shows that the Mu'tazila's denial of attributes contradicts their own names: Ahl al-Tawhid and Ahl al-'Adl.
Their denial makes God punish without crime, which is unjust.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبعد ذلك إما أن يقال : إن كلام الله تعالى قائم بذاته وهو متكلم آمر ناه كما قال أهل الحق - نصرهم الله - وإما أن ينكر أن يكون الله تعالى كلام وأمر ونهي وتبطل فرضية الإيمان والعبادات وخطر الكفر والمعاصي لانعدام أمره ونهيه، وهذا كفر ظاهر وضلال مبين. ثم لو كان الأمر كذلك لكان الله تعالى بتعذيب من جحده وصد عن سبيله وسفك الدماء وآثار الفتن والفساد، معذبا من لم يرتكب مأثما ولا اكتسب جريمة فيكون عندهم ظالما لا عادلا تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا. ثم هم بنفي الصفات يسمون أنفسهم أهل التوحيد يعنون أنهم يثبتون في الأزل موجودا واحدا، وقد أبطلوا عدل الله تعالى حيث صار معذبا من غير جريمة على ما قررت وبإثبات قدرة تخليق الأفعال للعباد يسمون أنفسهم أهل عدل، لأن
After that, either it is said: that the speech of God Most High is subsisting in His essence, and He is a speaker, commander, prohibiter, as the People of Truth—may God support them—say; or it is denied that God Most High has speech, command, and prohibition, and the obligation of faith and worship and the warning of disbelief and sins become void due to the absence of His command and prohibition, and this is manifest disbelief and clear misguidance. Then, if the matter were so, God Most High would be, by virtue of punishing the one who denies Him, turns away from His path, sheds blood, and causes [آثار] of sedition and corruption, punishing one who has committed no sin nor acquired any crime, so He would be, according to them, unjust, not just—exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. Then, by negating the attributes, they call themselves the People of Unity, meaning that they affirm in pre-eternity one existent, and they have nullified the justice of God Most High, since He would be a punisher without crime, as I have established; and by affirming the power of the servants to originate actions, they call themselves the People of Justice, because.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage presents two alternatives regarding God's speech. The first is to affirm that God's speech is an attribute subsisting in His essence (qa'im bi dhatihi), making Him a speaker, commander, and prohibiter, as the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) hold. The second is to deny that God has speech, command, and prohibition, which would nullify the obligations of faith and worship and the warnings of disbelief and sins because no command or prohibition would exist. This is manifest disbelief. The author then argues that if there were no command or prohibition, God's punishing those who reject Him, turn away from His path, and cause corruption would be punishing without any sin, making Him unjust. This is impossible for God. The author then criticizes the Mu'tazila: they negate attributes and call themselves 'People of Unity' (Ahl al-Tawhid) because they affirm one eternal existent, but they have undermined God's justice by making Him punish without crime. They also affirm that servants have the power to create their own actions (qudrat takhliq al-af'al) and call themselves 'People of Justice' (Ahl al-'Adl). The passage ends abruptly with 'because' (li-anna).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying God's attributes is necessary for true tawhid (unity).
Correction: Denying God's speech leads to logical consequences that make God unjust, thus contradicting tawhid and 'adl.

Checkpoint

C) The Qur'an would be created.
D) The Prophet would not be needed.

Key Takeaway
Affirming God's speech as an attribute is essential for faith and justice; denying it leads to logical contradictions and is tantamount to kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

If Speech Were Created in a Locus, the Locus Would Be the Speaker
The author proves that if God created speech in a locus, that locus would be the speaker, not God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributes belong to their locus, not to the originator of the locus, and how this applies to the divine attribute of speech.

Core Idea
Attributes characterize their direct locus, not the creator of that locus.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents a crucial argument in the debate over the nature of divine speech. By examining everyday examples, the author shows that if God were to create speech in a physical substrate, that substrate—not God—would be the speaker. This reasoning refutes certain theological positions that separate the attribute from the speaker.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the list of examples and the reference to Abu al-Hudhayl.
2
Identify the principle and examples
The passage begins by stating that an attribute belongs to its locus, not its originator. Then it gives many examples: dead, one-eyed, blind, etc. Understand how these examples illustrate the principle.
Each example shows a quality that inheres in a subject, not in the one who caused it.
3
Consider the extreme consequence
The author warns that misattributing these qualities to the originator would lead to disbelief. This underscores the seriousness of the principle.
4
Analyze the analogy from Abu al-Hudhayl
Abu al-Hudhayl's claim about faith is used as a parallel: if God creates faith in someone, that person is the believer, not God. This reinforces the principle.
The analogy directly supports the conclusion about speech.
5
Apply the conclusion to speech
The author concludes that if God created speech in a locus, that locus would be the speaker. This invalidates the three divisions (of kalam) that try to separate speech from God.
Reflect on why this matters for understanding God's attribute of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محدثه، والصفة تكون صفة لمحلها لا لمحدثها ألا ترى أن الميت والأعور والأعمى والأشل، والأعرج، والأسود والأبيض، والحلو والمر، والمتحرك والساكن محال هذه الصفات لا موجودها، ومن وصف موجود هذه الصفات بها تسارع الناس إلى إكفاره وقصدوا ضرب علاوته وإتلاف مهجته فكذا هذا. ولهذا زعم أبو الهذيل العلاف أن من خلق الله فيه الإيمان بطريق الجبر كان المؤمن هو المحل لا الله تعالى. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك علم أن الله تعالى ولو خلق الكلام في محل لكان المتكلم ذلك المحل لا الله تعالى. وظهر بهذا فساد الأقسام الثلاثة كلها.
Its originator, and the attribute is an attribute for its locus, not for its originator. Do you not see that the dead, the one-eyed, the blind, the paralyzed, the lame, the black, the white, the sweet, the bitter, the moving, and the stationary are loci of these attributes, not their existents. And whoever describes an existent of these attributes with them, people hasten to declare him an unbeliever and intend to strike his neck and destroy his life. Likewise this. And because of this, Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf claimed that if God creates faith in someone by way of compulsion, then the believer is the locus, not God. And if the matter is thus, then it is known that if God created speech in a locus, that locus would be the speaker, not God. And by this appears the corruption of all three divisions.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a proof that attributes belong to their locus, not to the creator of that locus. The author gives examples of physical and sensory attributes: deadness, one-eyedness, blindness, etc. These attributes characterize the substrate (the person or object) not the maker. If someone were to attribute these to the creator, people would brand him an unbeliever. Then the author references Abu al-Hudhayl's position on faith: if God creates faith in someone, that person is the believer, not God. By analogy, if God created speech in a locus, that locus would be the speaker. Thus the three divisions (presumably of kalam) are invalid.

محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speech can be created as a separate attribute without inhering in the speaker.
Correction: If speech were created in a locus, that locus would be the speaker, not God; therefore speech must inhere in the speaker Himself.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if God created speech in a locus, who would be the speaker?
A) God
B) The locus
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
If God created speech in a locus, that locus would be the speaker, not God; thus speech must be an attribute of God Himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you take pride in a righteous deed, do you recognize that it is truly God who created that action in you? How would your humility change if you consistently attributed every good within you to its true locus?
?
The author warns that misattributing an attribute to its originator rather than its locus leads to disbelief. Are there any attributes—such as success, knowledge, or beauty—that you wrongly claim as your own, forgetting that you are merely a locus? What would it cost you to correct that today?
?
If the Quran is the speech of God, not created in a locus, how does your relationship with it change when you recite it? Do you approach it as the very words of the Speaker, or as a mere created text?

The Necessity of God's Speech as an Eternal Attribute
The author argues that God's speech must be an attribute subsisting in His essence to avoid injustice and nullification of obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's speech must be an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, and the logical consequences of denying it.

Core Idea
Denying God's eternal speech leads to nullifying faith and accusing God of injustice.

This passage presents a crucial argument from the Maturidi tradition for the eternity of the divine attribute of speech. It uses a dichotomy to show that unless God's speech is an eternal attribute, the entire structure of religious obligation collapses and God would be unjust.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong either/or structure.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two alternatives: either God's speech is an attribute of His essence, or it is denied. Identify what each entails.
3
Understand the consequences of denial
If speech, command, and prohibition are denied, then faith and worship become non-obligatory, and disbelief loses its significance. Trace this logic.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the justice argument
The passage goes further: without command, punishment would be unjust. This shows that the denial leads to accusing God of injustice, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله آمرناه بصفة قائمة بذاته]: فبعد ذلك إما أن يقال : إن كلام الله تعالى قائم بذاته وهو متكلم آمر ناه كما قال أهل الحق - نصرهم الله - وإما أن ينكر أن يكون الله تعالى كلام وأمر ونهي وتبطل فرضية الإيمان والعبادات وخطر الكفر والمعاصي لانعدام أمره ونهيه، وهذا كفر ظاهر وضلال مبين. ثم لو كان الأمر كذلك لكان الله تعالى بتعذيب من جحده وصد عن سبيله وسفك الدماء وآثار الفتن والفساد، معذبا من لم يرتكب مأثما ولا اكتسب جريمة فيكون عندهم ظالما لا عادلا تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا.
God is commander and prohibitor by an attribute subsisting in His essence. So after that, either it is said that the speech of God Most High subsists in His essence, and He is a speaker, commander, prohibitor as the people of truth said – may God support them – or it is denied that God Most High has speech, command, and prohibition, and the obligatory nature of faith and acts of worship becomes void, and the threat of disbelief and sins [becomes void] due to the non-existence of His command and prohibition. And this is clear disbelief and manifest error. Then, if the matter were otherwise, God Most High, by torturing those who deny Him, turn away from His path, shed blood, and cause sedition and corruption, would be torturing one who has committed no sin nor acquired any crime, and thus He would be, according to them, unjust, not just. Exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, greatly exalted.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage presents a dichotomy: either God's speech is an attribute subsisting in His essence (the position of Ahl al-Haqq), or else His commands and prohibitions are denied, which nullifies the obligatory nature of faith and worship. This latter position is kufr. Moreover, if there were no command, then God punishing anyone would be unjust, because there is no prohibition to have been violated. So the Mu'tazili position leads to accusing God of injustice.

متكلم

كفر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's speech is a created entity separate from His essence.
Correction: The argument shows that if speech were not an eternal attribute, then command and prohibition would be non-existent, nullifying faith and implying injustice.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would become void if God's speech is not an attribute of His essence?
A) The existence of the world
B) The obligatory nature of faith and worship
C) God's knowledge
D) The prophethood

Key Takeaway
God's speech is an eternal attribute; denying it undermines religion and God's justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage warns that denying God's eternal speech leads to accusing Him of injustice. Have you ever, in a moment of pain or confusion, doubted God's justice? How does the logical necessity of God's speech and command confront that doubt in your heart?
?
Your very act of worship today is only valid because of an eternal command from Allah. Does your prayer feel like a response to that eternal reality, or is it mechanical? What would it mean for you to stand before Allah in prayer consciously aware that you are answering His eternal call?

Derived Names Refer to the Locus of Attributes
Al-Nasafi states the rule for derived names and illustrates with blackness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that derived names from attributes (such as 'speaker' from speech) refer to the entity that bears the attribute (the locus), not to the agent who originated it.

Core Idea
Derived names go to the bearer, not the maker.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by establishing a fundamental principle of kalam: when a name is derived from an attribute, it applies to the locus in which that attribute subsists, not to the agent who brings it about. He uses the example of blackness to illustrate this linguistic convention. This principle directly counters the Mu'tazili objection that 'mutakallim' should refer to God as the creator of speech, not to a locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly, noting the author's opening tone and the central claim.
Notice the author's rhetorical style in dismissing the opponent.
2
Identify the core claim
Locate the sentence: 'the derived names from attributes apply to the entity that is their locus, not to the one who originated them.' This is the key rule.
The author emphasizes this is by consensus of rational people.
3
Analyze the blackness analogy
Consider the analogy: God creates blackness in a locus. Who is obliged to call the locus 'black'? The only basis is linguistic convention, not a rational necessity.
This shows that derived names follow the locus, not the agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول له: من رزق أدنى لب وأكرم بنُبَذ من العلم يسيرة عرف حيده عن سنن الصواب، والاشتغال بما يدل على غاية حيرته ونهاية إفلاسه، فإن القائل لو قال: أنا لا أتكلم بهذه اللفظة بل أقول : إن الكلام يشتق منه اسم المتكلم بإجماع العقلاء والمشتق من الأسامي عن الصفات كان واقعا على الذات الذي هو محلها دون من أحدثها، واعتبر بما بينا من الصفات والأعراض ، وكذا كان الصفات صفات المحال دون فاعلها على ما بينا فالعدول عن هذا والتمسك بالوجوب، ومن يجب عليه أن يشتق وبأي سبب يجب؟ مغالطة بإرادة وحيرة ظاهرة. ويقال له: إذا خلق الله تعالى في محل سوادا فمن الذي وجب عليه أن يسمى المحل أسود؟ وأي دليل ورد في ذلك من دلائل السمع سوى الوضع الأصلي في اللسان أن الاسم المأخوذ من المعنى يكون واقعا على الذات الحامل له؟ فأي جواب أجاب به فهو الجواب له. وما ذكر أن الرائحة موجودة في الكافور ولم يشتق لمحلها اسم في غاية الفساد، فإنا نقول له: إن الكلام يشتق منه الاسم فيكون الاسم راجعا إلى محله دون موجوده، ولم نقل كل معنى وجد في محل ينبغي أن يشتق منه الاسم لا محالة ليلزمنا هذا السؤال بل قلنا : إذا اشتق منه اسم كان اسما لحامله دون موجوده وهذه لم يشتق منها اسم ثم يقال له: لو كان الله تعالى أعطى اسم المتكلم بتخليقه الكلام في محل لكان ينبغي أن يعطي له اسم مشتق من الرائحة لإيجاده الرائحة في الكافور، محل، فبأي جواب أجاب وفرق فهو فرقنا .وجوابنا. ثم نقول : إن كان لم يشتق للكافور من الرائحة الموجودة فيه اسم فالرائحة صفة للكافور أم صفة لموجدها وهو الله تعالى؟ فإن قال : صفة موجدها فكانت هى رائحة الله تعالى لا رائحة الكافور وأحد شطري هذا الكلام كفر . والثاني جحد الضروريات، وإن قال: هي صفة الكافور فنقول: فالكلام أيضا ينبغي أن يكون صفة المحل، فيكون كلام المحل لا كلام الله تعالى كرائحة الكافور، وسواد الغراب وبياض الثلج ونتن الجيفة ومرارة الخمر، وهذا بحمد الله واضح.
We say to him: Whoever is given the least understanding and honored with a few fragments of knowledge knows his deviation from the path of correctness, and his preoccupation with what indicates the utmost of his confusion and the end of his bankruptcy. For if someone said: 'I do not utter this word, but I say that speech derives the name 'speaker' by consensus of the rational, and derived names from attributes apply to the entity that is their locus, not to the one who originated them,' and consider what we have shown concerning attributes and accidents, and likewise attributes are attributes of the loci, not of their agent as we have shown—then turning away from this and clinging to obligation—who must derive such a name and by what cause must it be?—is a sophism with intent and apparent confusion. And it is said to him: When God Most High creates blackness in a locus, who is obliged to call that locus 'black'? And what proof from scriptural evidences has come concerning that other than the original linguistic convention that the name taken from a meaning applies to the entity that bears it? Whatever answer he gives, that is the answer for him. And what he mentioned that smell exists in camphor and no name was derived for its locus is extremely corrupt. For we say to him: Speech derives a name from it, so the name reverts to its locus, not to its existent. And we did not say that every meaning found in a locus must necessarily have a name derived from it so that this question would be incumbent on us; rather we said: When a name is derived from it, it is a name for its bearer, not for its existent, and this (the smell) has no name derived from it. Then it is said to him: If God Most High gave the name 'speaker' by His creating speech in a locus, it would be necessary that He give to it a name derived from the smell for His causing the smell to exist in the camphor, (its) locus. So by whatever answer he answers and differentiates, that is our differentiation and our answer. Then we say: If no name was derived for the camphor from the smell existing in it, is the smell an attribute of the camphor or an attribute of its originator, God Most High? If he says: 'An attribute of its originator,' then it would be the smell of God Most High, not the smell of the camphor, and one of the two sides of this statement is unbelief, and the second is denial of necessities. And if he says: 'It is an attribute of the camphor,' then we say: Speech too ought to be an attribute of the locus, so it would be the speech of the locus, not the speech of God Most High, like the smell of camphor, the blackness of the crow, the whiteness of snow, the stench of a carcass, and the bitterness of wine. And this, praise be to God, is clear.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) concerning the derivation of the name 'mutakallim' (speaker) from the attribute of kalam (speech). The author, al-Nasafi, argues that any derived name from an attribute applies to the locus (mahall) in which the attribute subsists, not to the agent who originated it. He begins by stating that anyone with minimal intelligence and knowledge would recognize this fact. He then considers a hypothetical objector who refuses to use the term 'kalam' but admits that speech derives the name 'mutakallim' by consensus. The author insists that derived names from attributes always refer to the entity bearing the attribute, not the one who brings it about. He then presents an analogy: if God creates blackness in a locus, no one is obliged to call that locus 'black' except by linguistic convention. The objector's reference to the camphor example—that camphor has a smell but no derived name—is faulty. The author clarifies that they do not claim that every attribute yields a derived name; rather, when a name is derived, it goes to the locus. He then pushes the objection to an absurd conclusion: if God gave the name 'mutakallim' by creating speech in a locus, then He would also have to give a name derived from the smell to the locus where He created the smell. He forces a dilemma: either the smell is an attribute of the camphor or of God. If of God, that is blasphemous; if of the camphor, then speech similarly must be an attribute of its locus (e.g., the physical speech organ), not of God. Thus the objection collapses. The passage ends with the author declaring the reasoning clear.

Key Terms
الكلام (al-kalam)
المشتق (al-mushtaq)
المتكلم (al-mutakallim)
الذات (al-dhat)
المحل (al-mahall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Derived names like 'speaker' refer to the agent who creates the attribute (e.g., God creates speech, so He is called 'speaker').
Correction: Derived names refer to the locus in which the attribute subsists, not to the agent. This is supported by the blackness analogy and linguistic convention.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a name is derived from an attribute, to whom does it apply?
A) The agent who originated the attribute
B) The locus that bears the attribute
C) Both equally
D) Neither, it is arbitrary

Key Takeaway
Derived names from attributes consistently apply to the locus in which the attribute subsists, not to the agent who originates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah 'the Speaker', do you unconsciously imagine a voice or speech organ? How does this lesson's principle—that derived names from attributes apply to the locus, not the originator—challenge that image? What will you do to purify your understanding of this name?
?
You often use derived names like 'teacher' (from teaching) or 'writer' (from writing). Do you apply them to the person or the action? How does this linguistic rule help you avoid mistakenly attributing divine qualities to created beings? Write one concrete way you will apply this awareness today.
?
If the name 'black' applies to the locus of blackness, not its creator, then what does that imply about attributing any derived name to Allah? Are you willing to surrender your own assumptions about how Allah's attributes relate to His essence? What step will you take to align your belief with this principle?

The Camphor Objection and the Dilemma of Attribution
Al-Nasafi refutes the camphor objection and presents a destructive dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the camphor objection by clarifying his position and then forcing a destructive dilemma that shows the contradiction in the Mu'tazili stance.

Core Idea
If smell is an attribute of camphor, then speech is an attribute of its locus.

In the second part of his refutation, Imam al-Nasafi addresses a specific objection concerning camphor and its smell. The opponent argues that since camphor has a smell but no derived name from it, the rule about derived names for loci is flawed. Al-Nasafi clarifies that he never claimed every attribute yields a derived name, only that when a name is derived, it goes to the locus. He then turns the objection into a powerful dilemma: either the smell is an attribute of God (blasphemy) or of the camphor (then speech likewise is an attribute of its locus).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Pay attention to the author's clarification and the structure of the dilemma.
The passage begins with the camphor objection.
2
Understand the clarification
Notice that al-Nasafi distinguishes between 'every meaning yields a derived name' (which he denies) and 'when a name is derived, it goes to the locus' (which he affirms).
This preempts the objection about camphor.
3
Analyze the turning of the tables
See how al-Nasafi turns the objection around: if God gave the name 'speaker' because He created speech in a locus, then He should also give a name derived from smell to the locus where He created smell. The opponent must differentiate consistently.
This is the key move.
4
Follow the dilemma
The author forces a choice: either the smell is an attribute of God (blasphemous) or of the camphor (then speech is of its locus). Both options refute the opponent.
The conclusion is that the opponent cannot escape.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) concerning the derivation of the name 'mutakallim' (speaker) from the attribute of kalam (speech). The author, al-Nasafi, argues that any derived name from an attribute applies to the locus (mahall) in which the attribute subsists, not to the agent who originated it. The author insists that derived names from attributes always refer to the entity bearing the attribute, not the one who brings it about. He then presents an analogy: if God creates blackness in a locus, no one is obliged to call that locus 'black' except by linguistic convention. The objector's reference to the camphor example—that camphor has a smell but no derived name—is faulty. The author clarifies that they do not claim that every attribute yields a derived name; rather, when a name is derived, it goes to the locus. He then pushes the objection to an absurd conclusion: if God gave the name 'mutakallim' by creating speech in a locus, then He would also have to give a name derived from the smell to the locus where He created the smell. The passage ends with the author declaring the reasoning clear.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lack of a derived name for camphor from its smell disproves the rule that derived names go to the locus of the attribute.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that the rule only applies when a name is actually derived; not every attribute yields a derived name. The camphor case does not undermine the rule, and the opponent's reasoning leads to a destructive dilemma.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does al-Nasafi present regarding the smell of camphor?
A) Either the smell is an attribute of the camphor or of God, both unacceptable to the opponent.
B) Either the smell is an attribute of the camphor or of the air, and the opponent cannot choose.
C) Either the smell is an attribute of God or of the speaker, and both are blasphemous.
D) Either the smell is an attribute of the camphor or of the speech, causing confusion.

Key Takeaway
The camphor objection is refuted by clarifying the conditional nature of derived names and by forcing a dilemma that shows the opponent's position leads to either blasphemy or the admission that speech is an attribute of a locus.

Objection of 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi on 'mutakallim'
Objection by 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi against speech subsisting in the speaker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili objection that if speech does not subsist in the speaker, the speaker cannot be called mutakallim, and the logical consequences 'Abd al-Jabbar derives from supposing it does.

Core Idea
Speech as an attribute vs. speech as an action leads to opposite definitions of 'mutakallim'.

This passage presents a key Mu'tazili objection from 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi against the Ash'ari/Maturidi view that God is called 'mutakallim' because speech subsists in Him. The objection challenges the very foundation of the Sunni understanding of divine speech. Read carefully to see how 'Abd al-Jabbar argues that if speech subsists, absurd consequences follow, and then consider why this objection was taken seriously.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Get a feel for the flow of the objection.
2
Identify the main premise
Find the initial premise: that a living being is called 'speaker' because he performs speech, not because speech subsists in him. Note the evidence he gives.
He says speech does not reside in the whole living body.
3
Trace the absurd consequences
Follow his chain of reasoning: if speech did subsist, then only the tongue would be speaker, truthfulness would not be attributed to the human, and multiple speakers would result.
These are intended to reduce the Sunni view to absurdity.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this objection is powerful. How would you answer it? Note that the text ends before the author's reply, highlighting the need for further study.
This sets up the next lesson on the Sunni response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض لعبد الجبار الرازي ومن جملة هذه الأسئلة ما ذكره عبد الجبار الرازي وهو عندهم أعلم أهل نحلته - فزعم أن الحي منا إنما كان متكلما لأنه فعل الكلام لا لأن الكلام قام به بدليل أن الكلام لا يحل جملة الحى. فكان يجب أن يستحيل أن يكون متكلما ، قال فإن قال : هلا كان كذلك بأن حل في بعضه قال: فجوابنا أنه كان يجب أن يكون اللسان متكلما دون الإنسان، لأن الكلام ببعضه أخص قال: وكان يجب أن لا يكون الصادق بالصدق الإنسان، ولا يتعلق الذم والمدح به، قال: وكان يجب أن يكون كل محل من اللسان متكلما إن كان المتكلم ما حل به الكلام، وكان يجب إذا كان كل حرف يختص بمحل منه أن لا يستقر كون شيء من ذلك متكلما
An objection of 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi. Among these questions is what 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi mentioned—and he is, in their view, the most learned of his sect—for he claimed that the living being among us is only called 'speaker' because he performs speech, not because speech subsists in him, as evidenced by the fact that speech does not reside in the whole living body. So it would be impossible for him to be a speaker. He said: If it is said: 'Why is it not the case that it resides in some part of it?' He said: Our answer is that then the tongue would have to be the speaker, not the human, because speech is specific to some part of him. He said: And it would be necessary that the truthful person is not (called) truthful by truthfulness, and praise and blame would not pertain to him. He said: And it would be necessary that every part of the tongue be a speaker if the speaker is that in which speech resides, and it would be necessary, if every letter is specific to a part of it, that none of that is settled to be a speaker.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi, a leading Mu'tazili scholar, against the Ash'ari/Maturidi view that God is called 'mutakallim' because speech subsists in Him (or in a locus). 'Abd al-Jabbar argues that a living being is only called 'speaker' because he performs the act of speaking, not because speech subsists in him, since speech does not reside in the entire living body. If speech did subsist in the body, he claims, it would be impossible for the whole human to be a speaker—only the part where speech resides would be speaker. He anticipates a counter-argument: what if speech resides in some part? He answers that then only that part (e.g., the tongue) would be speaker, not the human. This would also lead to absurd consequences: truthfulness would not be attributed to the human, and praise/blame would not apply to him. Furthermore, if every part of the tongue that contains a letter is considered the speaker, there would be multiple speakers. The passage ends mid-argument; the author's response is not included in this window, but the objection is laid out clearly to be refuted in the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
الحي (al-hayy)
الفعل (al-fi'l)
القيام (al-qiyam)
المحل (al-mahall)
اللسان (al-lisan)
الصادق (al-sadiq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi draws from the premise that speech does not subsist in the entire living body?
A) The speaker cannot be called 'mutakallim'
B) The tongue is the speaker, not the human
C) The human is the speaker because he creates speech
D) Speech does not exist

Key Takeaway
'Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi objects that if speech does not subsist in the speaker, calling a person 'mutakallim' is impossible; if it does subsist, absurd consequences follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you ever pause to consider what it means for God to 'speak'? Can you honestly claim that your concept of divine speech is free from imagining sounds or letters, as this objection attempts to refute?
?
If you were asked to defend the Ash'ari position that God's speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, could you refute 'Abd al-Jabbar's objection without relying on memorized arguments? What does that reveal about the depth of your conviction?
?
The objection forces us to ask: is our understanding of God's attributes based on reasoning that can withstand scrutiny, or is it merely inherited belief? How will you ensure that your tawhid is built on certainty, not blind acceptance?

Imam Nasafi's Dialectical and True Answer on Divine Speech
Nasafi presents a dialectical and true answer to the objection on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Nasafi's two-part refutation of the Mu'tazili objection that speech cannot reside in the entire divine essence, and you will learn the distinction between the earlier and later scholars of attributes regarding how an attribute applies to the essence.

Core Idea
The entire divine essence is described as speaking, based on the unity of God's essence.

Imam Nasafi responds to a Mu'tazili objector who argues that if speech does not reside in all parts of a being, the whole cannot be called a speaker. Nasafi first offers a dialectical answer exposing inconsistency in the opponent's position, then provides the true answer from the earlier scholars of attributes: the essence is one, and an attribute applies to the whole even if it inheres in a part.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the shift from dialectical to true answer.
2
Identify the dialectical argument
Find how Nasafi turns the objection back on the opponent. Note the key phrase 'هذا هو الجواب الجدلي'.
The dialectical answer shows the opponent's reasoning leads to self-contradiction.
3
Understand the true answer
Focus on the distinction between the earlier and later scholars of attributes. Why does the earlier view mean the whole essence is speaking?
The earlier view considers the essence as one unit by divine making.
4
Connect to Maturidi theology
Reflect on how this argument supports the Maturidi position that God's attributes are not separate from His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول في جوابه - والله الموفق [الرد على اعتراض عبد الجبار الرازي]: إنه جمع في كلامه أنواعا من الهذيان والمغالطة يظهر ذلك عند الكشف من ذلك أنه قال : إن الكلام لا يحل جملة الحي، فكان يجب أن يستحيل أن يكون كله متكلما. قلنا له والكلام لا يفعله جملة الحي، فإن جلده وعظمه وأحشاءه وأمعاءه وأصابعه وأظفاره لا توصف بتحصيلها الكلام ، فكان يستحيل أن يكون كله متكلما، ولما جاز له أن يجعل كل الذات متكلما وإن لم يفعل الكلام لما بينا من هذه الأجزاء مع أن المتكلم عنده فاعل الكلام ، لماذا لا يجوز لخصمه أن يجعل كل الذات متكلما وإن لم يقم الكلام بجميع أجزائه ؟ فيلزمه عين ما ألزم خصمه، والتعلق بما يفضي ببطلان مذهبه حسب قضائه ببطلان مذهب خصمه درب من السفه وعدول عن سنن المجادلة. هذا هو الجواب الجدلي.

فأما حقيقة الجواب فهو ما مر في مسألة الصفات من بيان الاختلاف بين قدماء أصحاب الصفات وبين متأخريهم، فإن عند القدماء كل الذات يكون موصوفا وإن وجدت الصفة ببعضه لما أن كل الذات جعل شيئا واحدا بجعل جاعل، وعند المتأخرين الموصوف بالصفة الجزء الذي قامت به الصفة إلا أنهم يضمرون ذكر ذلك الجزء عند وضوح المعنى، بأن كانت العادة جارية أن تلك الصفة لا توجد إلا بجزء مخصوص، ولو كانت توجد بكل جزء لم يكن بد من ذكر ذلك الجزء على ما قررنا هناك كذا هذا، ثم ما ذكر بعد هذا كله بناء على مذهبه أن الكلام هو الحروف المنظومة وأنه قائم باللسان، وما ذكر أن الكلام لو وجد بجزء من أجزاء اللسان ينبغي أن يكون المتكلم ذلك الجزء من اللسان أو اللسان كله، لأنه أخص به منه بجميع البدن، وقد مر جوابه أن على أحد المذهبين كل البدن جعل كشيء واحد لا يتجزأ، وجعل كل معنى ترجع منفعته إلى كل البدن قائما كله بكله، وعلى المذهب الثاني اختص الجزء الذي هو محل الصفة بالاسم المشتق من الصفة، على أن هذا ممنوع فإن الكلام ليس بقائم باللسان على ما تبين وبهذا يعرف بطلان ما ذكر أنه إذا كان كل حرف يختص بمخرج على حدة ومحل مخصوص ينبغي أن لا يستقر كون كل جزء من ذلك متكلما ، لأن الحرف الواحد لا يكون كلاما، فإنا بينا أن الكلام ليس هو الحروف وليس بقائم باللسان ليمكن التعلق بهذا.

ثم نقول له: هذا الكلام ليس بلازم على خصمك لما مر، وهو عليك لازم جدا، فإن مذهبك أن الكلام هو الحروف المنظومة ولا بقاء للكلام عندك فإن تقتفي آثار إمامك أبي هاشم الذي بقدمك يوم القيامة فيوردك النار. ومن رأيه أن الكلام من قبيل ما لا يبقى من الأعراض، فإذا عندك لا بقاء لشيء من هذه الحروف.
We say in answer to him—and God is the grantor of success—[Refutation of the objection of Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi]: He has gathered in his speech types of raving and fallacy; that becomes clear upon investigation. Among that is his saying: Speech does not reside in the entire living being, so it must be impossible for the whole to be a speaker. We say to him: Speech is not performed by the entire living being; his skin, bone, entrails, intestines, fingers, and nails are not described as producing speech, so it would be impossible for the whole to be a speaker. And since he allows himself to make the entire essence a speaker even though it does not perform speech, because of these parts we mentioned—while according to him the speaker is the agent of speech—why does he not allow his opponent to make the entire essence a speaker even though speech does not subsist in all its parts? So the very same [dilemma] he imposed on his opponent befalls him. Holding onto that which leads to the invalidity of his own doctrine, just as he judged the invalidity of his opponent's doctrine, is a kind of foolishness and a departure from the path of debate. This is the dialectical answer. As for the true answer, it is what has passed in the question of the attributes regarding the clarification of the difference between the earlier proponents of attributes and the later ones. For according to the earlier ones, the entire essence is described even if the attribute exists in a part, because the entire essence is made one thing by the making of a Maker. According to the later ones, the part in which the attribute subsists is the one described by the attribute, except that they imply the mention of that part when the meaning is clear, if custom dictates that that attribute exists only in a specific part. If it existed in every part, there would be no alternative but to mention that part, as we established there. This is the same here. Then what he mentioned after all this is based on his doctrine that speech is composed letters and that it subsists in the tongue. And he mentioned that if speech existed in a part of the parts of the tongue, the speaker ought to be that part of the tongue or the whole tongue, because it is more specific to it than to the whole body. Its answer has already passed: according to one of the two schools, the whole body is made as one indivisible thing, and every meaning whose benefit returns to the whole body is made to subsist in the whole by the whole; according to the second school, the part that is the locus of the attribute is specified for the name derived from the attribute. However, this is prohibited, for speech does not subsist in the tongue, as has been clarified, and by this the nullification of what he mentioned is known: that if every letter is specific to a separate point of articulation and a specific place, it should not be settled that every part of that is a speaker, because a single letter is not speech. For we clarified that speech is not letters and does not subsist in the tongue such that one could hold on to this. Then we say to him: This speech does not necessarily follow your opponent, for what has passed, and it is necessarily required of you very much. For your doctrine is that speech is composed letters and speech has no duration according to you. So you follow the traces of your imam Abu Hashim, who will go before you on the Day of Resurrection and lead you into the Fire. His view is that speech is of the category of accidents that do not endure. So according to you, there is no duration for any of these letters.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Imam Nasafi of an objection raised by the Mu'tazili scholar Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi regarding the nature of divine speech and how it relates to the essence of God. The objection is that speech does not reside in the entire living being, so the whole being cannot be a speaker. Nasafi first provides a dialectical (jadali) answer, then the true answer (haqiqa) based on earlier discussions about the attributes. In the dialectical part, he exposes inconsistency: the opponent admits that the entire essence is a speaker even though not all parts perform speech (like skin, bones), so the opponent's objection applies to himself. Then he gives the true answer by distinguishing earlier and later scholars of attributes: according to early scholars, the entire essence is described by an attribute even if the attribute is only in a part, because the essence is made one by God; later scholars say the part that bears the attribute is described, but they imply the whole for clarity. Then he addresses the specific claim that speech is letters on the tongue, stating that speech does not subsist on the tongue. Finally, he turns the argument against the opponent, noting that the opponent's own doctrine (that speech is composed letters that do not endure) leads to consequences that the opponent must accept, ultimately leading him to follow Abu Hashim into the Fire. The passage thus defends the Maturidi position that God's entire essence is described as speaking, using both logical and theological arguments.

Key Terms

متكلم
جواب جدلي
حقيقة الجواب
قدماء أصحاب الصفات
متأخروهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech is exactly like human speech composed of letters and sounds, requiring a physical locus.
Correction: Nasafi explains that in God, the entire essence is speaking in a way that transcends physical loci, based on the unity of the divine essence.

Checkpoint
According to the earlier scholars of attributes, how does the attribute of speech apply to the divine essence?
A) Only the part where speech inheres is described.
B) The entire essence is described because it is one.
C) Only the tongue is described.
D) Speech does not apply to the essence.

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi defends the Maturidi view that God's entire essence is described as speaking, using a dialectical rebuttal and a deeper theological principle about divine unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's speech (the Qur'an), do you imagine it as something separate from His essence? Are you willing to accept that His entire being is described as speaking, even if your mind cannot fully grasp it? What change in your heart does this demand?
?
Nasafi exposes contradictions in the opponent's argument. Are there contradictions in your own understanding of God's attributes that you have been ignoring, such as treating His mercy as less essential than His power? Will you seek clarity today?
?
If God's entire essence is described as 'speaking,' then every word of the Qur'an carries the weight of His whole being. How does this affect the way you approach its verses? Will you let this truth transform your recitation and reflection?

Refuting the Mu'tazili View of Speech as Letters on the Tongue
Nasafi refutes the claim that speech is letters on the tongue and warns against Mu'tazili doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Nasafi's arguments against the Mu'tazili claim that divine speech is composed of letters subsisting on the tongue, and you will grasp the ultimate consequence of the Mu'tazili doctrine of speech's non-duration.

Core Idea
Divine speech is not letters on a tongue; it subsists in God's essence.

The Mu'tazili Abd al-Jabbar al-Razi argues that if speech is letters on the tongue, then only the tongue or a part of it would be the speaker. Nasafi refutes this by reiterating that speech is not letters and does not subsist in the tongue. He then turns the argument on the opponent: since their imam Abu Hashim held that speech is a non-enduring accident, the opponent's own doctrine leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully, noting the shift to the opponent's argument about the tongue and Nasafi's response.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
Understand the claim: if speech subsists in a part of the tongue, then that part or the whole tongue should be the speaker.
The opponent bases this on the idea that speech is letters on the tongue.
3
Analyze Nasafi's responses
Nasafi gives two main responses: 1) The principle of unifying the body for attributes, 2) The refutation that speech is not on the tongue at all.
4
Grasp the final warning
Nasafi concludes by warning that the Mu'tazili doctrine leads to following Abu Hashim into Hell, highlighting the gravity of the error.
This is a rhetorical punch grounded in theological consequence.

Nasafi's Reply to the Mu'tazila Objection on Speech as a Quality of the Locus
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila objection about speech being a quality of the agent, not the locus, and gives his reply with a charge of inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila argument that speech cannot be an attribute of an inanimate locus and exposes their inconsistency regarding the creation of human actions.

Core Idea
Speech can be attributed to a locus if the locus can receive it, regardless of agency.

The Mu'tazila objected that speech, when found in an inanimate object like a bush, cannot be an attribute of the object because the object cannot speak; it must belong to the agent (God). Nasafi responds forcefully, calling this 'pure deception' and arguing that the only criterion is whether the attribute can subsist in the locus. He also points out a fatal inconsistency in their own doctrine on the creation of human actions. This passage sharpens our understanding of how Maturidis defend the divine speech attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the Mu'tazila's condition for attribute attribution: if a locus can be described by the attribute, it belongs to the locus; if not, it belongs to the agent.
They apply this to speech: a living being can be described as speaking, but an inanimate object cannot.
2
Identify Nasafi's counter-argument
Notice how Nasafi shifts the criterion from 'possibility of description' to 'possibility of subsistence'. If the attribute can exist in the locus, description is possible.
He says the impossibility of description only arises if the attribute cannot exist there at all.
3
Examine the inconsistency
See how Nasafi uses the Mu'tazila's own argument on the creation of human actions to show they contradict themselves. They claim God would be called a disbeliever for creating disbelief, but they ignore that the human locus can also be called a disbeliever.
They do not apply the same condition they propose for speech to human actions.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Understand that this defense preserves the reality of God's speech as an eternal attribute inherent to His essence, not merely a created effect.

ومن جملة هذه الأسئلة ما ذكره بعض رؤسائهم أن الصفة إذا وجدت في محل لا يمتنع اتصاف المحل بها كانت صفة للمحل ، والاسم المشتق كل اسما للمحل كما في سائر الصفات والأعراض التي ذكرتم ، وإذا وجدت في محل، يمتنع اتصاف المحل بها، ما كانت صفة للمحل، وما كان الاسم المشتق منها اسما له، بل كانت صفة لفاعلها ، والاسم المشتق منها اسما له لا للمحل، فالكلام إذا وجد في الحي كان صفة للحي، والمتكلم به كان هو المحل لأن الحي لا يستحيل أن يكون متكلما فأما إذا وجد في الجماد [؟] فقد كان صفة للفاعل والمتكلم به هو الفاعل، لاستحالة كون الجماد [؟] والموات متكلما [؟]. فنقول في جوابه: إن هذا تمويه محض وهذيان ظاهر فإن القول بامتناع اتصاف المحل بصفة ما إنما يكون لامتناعه قبول [؟] تلك الصفة واستحالة وجودها فيه، فأما إذا لم يستحل وجودها فيه لم يستحل الاتصاف ولم يمتنع، بل صار الاتصاف واستحقاق الاسم المشتق له ثابتا ضرورة لقبوله الصفة فلو لم يستحل قيام الكلام بالجماد ولم يمتنع حصوله فيه لم يمتنع [؟] اتصافه البتة بل صار الاسم له مستحقا كاسم الأسود والأبيض والحي والميت لما قبل هذه الصفات من الذوات، ثم إن المتمسك بهذا النوع من السؤال ناقض أعظم أصل لهم، فإنهم يزعمون في مسألة خلق الأفعال أن الله تعالى لو كان هو الخالق لأفعال العباد، لكان هو الكافر لخلق الكفر والزانى بخلق الزنا، وإن كان ما خلق فيه هذه الأفعال من أشخاص الممتحنين صالحا للاتصاف بهذه الصفات فلم يكن استحقاقه الاسم المشتق من هذه الصفات ممتنعا لأنهم [؟] لم يراعوا هذا الشرط بل أطلقوه إطلاقا، وعند لزوم التناقض وتعذر الفرق بين هذه الصفات وبين ما قدمنا ذكره من الصفات كالألوان والطعوم وغيرها
And among these questions is what one of their leaders mentioned: that the attribute when it is found in a locus and it is not impossible for the locus to be described by it, it is an attribute of the locus, and the derived noun is a name for the locus, as in all other attributes and accidents that you have mentioned. But when it is found in a locus and it is impossible for the locus to be described by it, it is not an attribute of the locus, and the derived noun is not a name for it; rather it is an attribute of its agent, and the derived noun is a name for it, not for the locus. So speech, when it is found in a living being, is an attribute of the living being, and the speaker is the locus because the living being is not impossible to be a speaker. But when it is found in an inanimate object, then it is an attribute of the agent, and the speaker is the agent, because it is impossible for the inanimate and the lifeless to be a speaker. We say in its answer: This is pure deception and manifest nonsense. For the statement about the impossibility of the locus being described by an attribute is only due to its inability to accept that attribute and the impossibility of its existence in it. But when its existence in it is not impossible, then being described is not impossible nor prevented; rather description and entitlement to the derived noun become established necessarily because of its acceptance of the attribute. So if the subsistence of speech in the inanimate were not impossible and its occurrence in it were not prevented, then its being described would not be prevented at all; rather the noun would become deserved for it, like the names black, white, living, and dead, because these attributes are received by the essences. Then the one who adheres to this type of question contradicts their greatest principle, for in the issue of the creation of actions they claim that if Allah were the creator of the actions of servants, then He would be the disbeliever for creating disbelief and the adulterer for creating adultery, even though the individuals of those tested in whom He created these actions are suitable to be described by these attributes, so the entitlement to the derived noun from these attributes would not be prevented. But they did not observe this condition but applied it absolutely, and the contradiction becomes necessary and the distinction between these attributes and what we have mentioned of attributes like colors, tastes, and others becomes impossible.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila and Nasafi's reply. The objector argues: If an attribute exists in a locus and the locus can be described by it, then the attribute belongs to the locus (e.g., whiteness in a white object). But if the locus cannot be described by it (e.g., a lifeless body cannot speak), then the attribute belongs to the agent that produced it. They apply this to speech: when speech is in a living being, the living being is the speaker; but when it is in an inanimate object (like the burning bush), the inanimate cannot be the speaker, so the speech must belong to God as the agent. Nasafi replies sharply that this is mere deception. The only reason a locus cannot be described by an attribute is if the attribute cannot possibly exist in that locus at all. But if it can exist, there is no impossibility. So if speech could subsist in an inanimate object (and they have not proven it impossible), then the inanimate could be described as speaking. He then points out their inconsistency: in the debate on the creation of human actions, they argue that if God creates disbelief, He would be called a disbeliever (because the agent is the creator). But they ignore that the human locus (the servant) is also capable of being described as a disbeliever. They do not apply the same condition they propose here, revealing their contradiction.

المحل
الفاعل
الاسم المشتق
خلق الأفعال
الجماد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speech cannot be an attribute of an inanimate object because the object cannot speak.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the only condition for attributing speech to a locus is the possibility of its subsistence in that locus, not the locus's ability to speak. If speech can exist in an inanimate object, the object can be described as speaking.

Checkpoint
What inconsistency does Nasafi point out in the Mu'tazila argument?
A) They apply different standards to speech and to human actions.
B) They deny that God can speak.
C) They accept that inanimate objects can speak.
D) They reject the use of derived nouns.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila objection by showing that the only barrier to attributing speech to a locus is its inability to receive it, not the impossibility of description, and exposes their inconsistency in the creation of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazila's inconsistency: they apply one rule to speech and another to actions. In your own understanding of Allah's attributes, are there areas where you hold inconsistent views? What would it cost you to bring them into harmony with the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that an inanimate object cannot be described as speaking. But Nasafi says if Allah can make speech subsist in it, it can be described accordingly. Do you truly believe that Allah has absolute power over all things, including making a tree or a rock speak? If yes, why do you sometimes limit His power in your heart when thinking about miracles?
?
Nasafi says the only barrier to description is the inability to receive. Reflect: Is there any attribute of Allah that you have considered impossible for Him to bestow upon a creature? How does this lesson challenge your assumptions about what Allah can do, and what practical change in your 'aqeedah does this demand?

Divine Attributes

Refuting the Mu'tazili Objection on Speech as an Attribute
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili objection on speech as an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili objection that speech cannot be an attribute of a subject, and how he establishes the eternity of God's speech.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazili objection collapses on its own inconsistency.

The Mu'tazila argued that speech cannot be an attribute of a subject (maḥall) but only of the agent. In this passage, Nasafi dismantles that objection, first exposing it as pure obfuscation, then pointing out a glaring contradiction in the Mu'tazili's own doctrine of the creation of actions, and finally giving an analogy from a sleeping person's speech to reinforce his point. This refutation not only defends the attribute of speech but also leads to the conclusion that God's speech is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili objection
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Nasafi presents the Mu'tazili position: speech that occurs in a living being is an attribute of the being, but speech in an inanimate object is attributed to the agent, not the object.
Notice the distinction based on whether the subject can be a speaker.
2
Understand Nasafi's initial response
Now read Nasafi's reply: the key is whether the attribute can possibly exist in the subject. If it can, description is possible. He gives examples like 'black' and 'white'.
This is a fundamental logical point.
3
Analyze the contradiction
Nasafi then shows that the Mu'tazili contradict their own principle: they claim that if God created disbelief, He would be called a disbeliever—yet they deny that speech can be an attribute of a subject even when it can exist there.
This exposes their inconsistency.
4
Grasp the proof of eternity
Finally, Nasafi argues that if God's speech were created, it would be subject to division, which is impossible, so it must be eternal. Read the concluding lines carefully.
This is the positive assertion of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحل بها، ما كانت صفة للمحل، وما كان الاسم المشتق منها اسما له، بل كانت صفة لفاعلها، والاسم المشتق منها اسما له لا للمحل، فالكلام إذا وجد في الحي كان صفة للحي، والمتكلم به كان هو المحل لأن الحي لا يستحيل أن يكون متكلما فأما إذا وجد في الجماد فقد كان صفة للفاعل والمتكلم به هو الفاعل، لاستحالة كون الجماد والموات متكلما.
الرد على اعتراض المعتزلة:
فنقول في جوابه: إن هذا تمويه محض وهذيان ظاهر فإن القول بامتناع اتصاف المحل بصفة ما إنما يكون لامتناعه قبول تلك الصفة واستحالة وجودها فيه، فأما إذا لم يستحل وجودها فيه لم يستحل الاتصاف ولم يمتنع، بل صار الاتصاف واستحقاق الاسم المشتق له ثابتا ضرورة لقبوله الصفة فلو لم يستحل قيام الكلام بالجماد ولم يمتنع حصوله فيه لم يمتنع اتصافه البتة بل صار الاسم له مستحقا كاسم الأسود والأبيض والحي والميت لما قبل هذه الصفات من الذوات، ثم إن المتمسك بهذا النوع من السؤال ناقض أعظم أصل لهم، فإنهم يزعمون في مسألة خلق الأفعال أن الله تعالى لو كان هو الخالق لأفعال العباد، لكان هو الكافر لخلق الكفر والزانى بخلق الزنا، وإن كان ما خلق فيه هذه الأفعال من أشخاص الممتحنين صالحا للاتصاف بهذه الصفات فلم يكن استحقاقه الاسم المشتق من هذه الصفات ممتنعا لأنهم لم يراعوا هذا الشرط بل أطلقوه إطلاقا، وعند لزوم التناقض وتعذر الفرق بين هذه الصفات وبين ما قدمنا ذكره من الصفات كالألوان والطعوم وغيرها من أنواع الأعراض، تمسكوا بهذا النوع من التمويه، وكل ذلك من نتائج الحيرة عند العدول عن اتباع الدليل وسلوك ما وضح من السبيل، وكذا النائم يتكلم بضرب من الكلام صحيح ويقرأ آيات من القرآن على وجهها ولم يعد ذلك فعلا له، لاستحالة وجود الفعل المحكم المتقن ممن لا علم له، والنوم مناف للعلم ولم يعد ذلك أحد كلام الله تعالى فدل أن ما قابله باطل بإجماع العقلاء، ويثبت بما ذكرت من دخول وجود كلام الله تعالى لو كان مخلوقا في حيز الامتناع لامتناع أقسامه التي ينقسم إليها لو كان محدثا، وإذا بطل ذلك ثبت كونه أزليا كما ادعينا نحن. والله الموفق.
The subject [is such that] what was an attribute of the subject, and the derived name from it is a name for it, but rather it was an attribute of its agent, and the derived name from it is a name for it, not for the subject. So speech, when it occurs in the living being, is an attribute of the living being, and the speaker by it is the subject, because the living being is not impossible to be a speaker. But when it occurs in the inanimate, then it is an attribute of the agent, and the speaker by it is the agent, due to the impossibility of the inanimate and dead being a speaker. Refutation of the Mu'tazilite objection: We say in reply: This is pure obfuscation and manifest nonsense. For the statement that a subject cannot be described by an attribute is only because of its impossibility to accept that attribute and the impossibility of its existence in it. But if its existence in it is not impossible, then description is not impossible and is not prevented; rather, description and entitlement to the derived name become established necessarily due to its acceptance of the attribute. So if the subsistence of speech in the inanimate were not impossible and its occurrence in it were not prevented, then its description would not be prevented at all; rather, the name becomes entitled to it, like the name 'black' and 'white' and 'living' and 'dead' when these attributes are accepted by the essences. Then the one who clings to this type of question contradicts their greatest principle: for they claim in the issue of the creation of actions that if God were the creator of the acts of servants, He would be the disbeliever for creating disbelief and the adulterer for creating adultery, even though what He created these acts in, from the individuals tested, is fit for description with these attributes. So the entitlement to the derived name from these attributes was not prevented, because they did not observe this condition but applied it absolutely. And when the contradiction became necessary and the distinction between these attributes and what we mentioned previously of attributes like colors and tastes and other types of accidents became impossible, they clung to this type of obfuscation. All of that is from the results of bewilderment when turning away from following the proof and taking the clear path. Likewise, the sleeping person speaks a type of correct speech and recites verses of the Quran in their proper form, yet that is not considered an act of his, due to the impossibility of the occurrence of sound, precise action from one who has no knowledge, and sleep negates knowledge. And no one considered that to be the speech of God Almighty. So it indicates that what opposes it is false by consensus of the rational people. And it is established by what I mentioned that the existence of God's speech, if it were created, falls into the realm of impossibility due to the impossibility of the divisions into which it would be divided if it were originated. And when that is invalidated, its being eternal is established, as we claim. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite objection that speech cannot be an attribute of the subject (maḥall) but only of the agent. The author first presents the Mu'tazilite position: if speech exists in a living being, it is an attribute of that living being, and the speaker is the subject itself because a living being can be a speaker. But if speech exists in an inanimate object, it is attributed to the agent who produced it, not to the object, because an object cannot be a speaker. Then the author answers: This is pure obfuscation. The principle that a subject cannot be described by an attribute holds only if the attribute cannot possibly exist in that subject. If it can exist there, then description is possible and the derived name (e.g., 'speaker') belongs to the subject by virtue of its receptivity. For example, we call a thing 'black' or 'white' because it accepts those colors. Similarly, if speech could subsist in an inanimate object, it would be described as 'speaking'. The author then points out a contradiction: The Mu'tazila themselves, in the issue of the creation of actions, argue that if God created disbelief, He would be called a disbeliever, and if He created adultery, He would be called an adulterer, even though the human subjects are capable of being described by those actions. Thus they inconsistently apply their own condition. Furthermore, they cannot distinguish between attributes like colors and speech in terms of the subject's receptivity. The author then gives an example: a sleeping person may speak correct words and recite Quran, but this is not considered his action because sleep negates knowledge, and a sound action cannot come from one without knowledge. Yet no one attributes that speech to God. This shows that their opposing view is false. Finally, the author argues that if God's speech were created, it would be subject to division and change, which is impossible, so it must be eternal. The lesson follows the argumentative order: objection, answer with principles, counterexample from their own doctrine, analogy with the sleeper, and concluding proof of eternity.
